The Physics Classroom = Topics
1-D=20 Kinematics
The motion of objects in one-dimension are described = using=20 words, diagrams, numbers, graphs, and = equations.
New= ton's=20 Laws
Newton's three laws of motion are explained and their=20 application to the analysis of the motion of objects in one = dimension is=20 discussed.
Vector= s -=20 Motion and Forces in Two Dimensions
Vector principles and operations are introduced and = combined=20 with kinematic principles and Newton's laws to describe, explain = and=20 analyze the motion of objects in two dimensions. Applications = include=20 riverboat problems, projectiles, inclined planes, and static=20 equilibrium.
Momen= tum=20 and Its Conservation
The impulse-momentum change theorem and the law of=20 conservation of momentum are introduced, explained and applied = to the=20 analysis of collisions of objects.
Work,= =20 Energy, and Power
Concepts of work, kinetic energy and potential energy = are=20 discussed; these concepts are combined with the work-energy = theorem to=20 provide a convenient means of analyzing an object or system of = objects=20 moving between an initial and final state.
Circu= lar=20 Motion and Satellite Motion
Newton's laws of motion and kinematic principles are = applied=20 to describe and explain the motion of objects moving in circles; = specific applications are made to roller coasters and athletics. = Newton's Universal Law of Gravitation is then presented and = utilized to=20 explain the circular and elliptical motion of planets and=20 satellites.
Einstein's=20 Theory of Special Relativity
The two postulates of Einstein's theory of special=20 relativity are introduced and applied to explain several motion=20 phenomenon which are only observable at relativistic speeds. The = twin=20 trip, spacetime travel, time dilation, and length contraction = are among=20 the topics discussed.
Static=20 Electricity
Basic principles of electrostatics are introduced in = order=20 to explain how objects become charged and to describe the effect = of=20 those charges on other objects in the neighboring surroundings. = Charging=20 methods, electric field lines and the importance of lightning = rods on=20 homes are among the topics discussed in this = unit.
Current=20 Electricity
The flow of charge through electric circuits is = discussed in=20 detail. The variables which cause and hinder the rate of charge = flow are=20 explained and the mathematical application of electrical = principles to=20 series, parallel and combination circuits is = presented.
Waves
The nature, properties and behaviors of waves are = discussed=20 and illustrated; the unique nature of a standing wave is = introduced and=20 explained.
Sound = Waves and Music
The nature of sound as a longitudinal, mechanical = pressure=20 wave is explained and the properties of sound are discussed. = Wave=20 principles of resonance and standing waves are applied in an = effort to=20 analyze the physics of musical instruments.
Light = Waves and Color
The behavior of light waves is introduced and = discussed;=20 polarization, color, diffraction and interference are introduced = as=20 supporting evidence of the wave nature of light. Color = perception is=20 discussed in detail.
Reflec= tion=20 and the Ray Model of Light
The ray nature of light is used to explain how light=20 reflects off of planar and curved surfaces to produce both real = and=20 virtual images; the nature of the images produced by plane = mirrors,=20 concave mirrors, and convex mirrors is thoroughly=20 illustrated.
Refrac= tion=20 and the Ray Model of Light
The ray nature of light is used to explain how light=20 refracts at planar and curved surfaces; Snell's law and = refraction=20 principles are used to explain a variety of real-world = phenomena;=20 refraction principles are combined with ray diagrams to explain = why=20 lenses produce images of objects.
 
Credits and Thanks
The Physics Classroom began as a = volunteer project during the 1996-97 school year. The = first five=20 units were completed during that time; no special funding = or=20 release time was provided for that work. During the summer of = 1998, a=20 grant was received from The First in the = World=20 Consortium of Schools in order to complete some additional = lessons=20 on the topics of waves, sound, light, reflection and refraction. = Thanks=20 to the generosity and support of the Consortium, an additional = five=20 units were added to The Physics Classroom during the summer of = 1998. At=20 that time, fellow colleague Brian Wegley contributed a unit on=20 Einstein's Theory of Special Relativity. Funds for the = production of=20 units on the topics of static and current electricity are = presently=20 being sought.=20 
Many of the graphics used in these lessons were=20 originally part of the "Hewitt DrewIt" clip art collection = available=20 through LaserPoint Educational Software (424 Quartz Street, = Redwood=20 City, CA 94062; Fax: 650-369-1220). The graphics were = subsequently=20 imported into a graphics program, edited, colorized, resized, = and used=20 in these Web documents. They are used with the permission of the = original artist - Paul Hewitt.
 
Technical Notes:
The lessons in The Physics Classroom contain a wealth = of=20 informative graphics. Some of the pages have several graphics; = all of=20 the pages have some graphics. The lessons would be almost = meaningless=20 and certainly lacking in effectiveness if viewed with a = text-only=20 browser. If using a 14.4 kb/s modem, be patient with some pages, = for=20 they may require up to 30 seconds to load. For the convenience = of users=20 of The Physics Classroom, estimated download times for users = with a 28.8=20 kBps modem have been noted in parenthesis.
Future Plans
Creating good and thorough web = resources=20 is a time-consuming task. As a classroom teacher, the daily = preparation=20 of lessons and labs, helping students, and (unfortunately) = grading=20 papers must come first. Nonetheless, my commitment to (and = pleasure in)=20 creating useful student resources remains high. Hopefully, = editing (a=20 tedious and unenjoyable chore) will be completed by the end of = Spring,=20 2000, leaving some time during the summer months for further=20 development. It is hoped that a series of "Advanced Topic" = sections will=20 be added to various units within The Physics Classroom. Such = topics=20 would include Young's Experiment, Thin Slit Interference = Patterns, Ray=20 Diagrams for Right Angle Mirrors, Acceleration-Time Graphs, etc. = At that=20 time, there are likely to be some minor interface changes = (rollovers,=20 improved navigation bar, etc.) and facelift changes (removal of = the red=20 color in the navigation bar which is rendered as a very deep red = on some=20 PC monitors) made at that time. 
Funds (in the form of a small grant) = are being=20 sought to fund the creation of some materials on the topics = static=20 electricity and current electricity. These units would assume a = similar=20 format and writing style as the currently existing = units.
Plans for the distant future include = making The=20 Physics Classroom more interactive by incorporating the content = into a=20 Shockwave Flash format. By so doing, animation and Check Your=20 Understanding sections can be seamlessly incorporated into the = content.=20 Furthermore, a method for checking (and recording) student = progress=20 through a unit would be possible. Such records can be kept on a = web=20 database and used to hold students accountable for doing the=20 reading.
Final plans include placing the files = at one or=20 more non-local sites. Placing them on a server other than our = Glenbrook=20 South server will likely improve reliability and make them = perpetually=20 assessable. A .cgi for transferring users to that external site = would be=20 made available. Once done, the same files residing at the GBS = server=20 would become password protected while the other duplicate files = would be=20 for public use. There are no plans to ever prevent the files = from being=20 used by other schools; it is only hoped that the reliability of = our=20 school's server is not jeopardized by the large amount of = external=20 traffic.
